Specifying the Crop in Land Lease: Validity and Cure of Ignorance
Ruling and reasoning on crop specification in land lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on crop specification in land lease and identify the circumstances that render the contract valid or voidable.

Core Idea
If the crop is unspecified, the land lease is fasid but becomes sahih once the lessee sows.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains a key rule in ijarah: the need to specify the crop or delegate choice to avoid ignorance (jahalah). If unspecified, the lease is initially voidable (fasid) but is cured by performance. We will explore the reasoning, analogies, and the difference between lease and sale regarding ancillary rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is leasing land for cultivation. The ruling: the lease is valid if the crop is specified or delegation is given; otherwise it is fasid but becomes sahih upon sowing.
Note the initial and final status.
2
Understand the problem: jahalah
The reason for fasid status is ignorance (jahalah) about which crop will be sown, as crops differ in soil impact and can cause disputes.
Hanafi rule: jahalah leading to dispute invalidates the contract.
3
Trace the cure by performance
If the lessee sows without specification, the ignorance is removed because the actual crop becomes known. The contract then becomes sahih and the named rent (musamma) is due.
The cure is unique to Hanafi fiqh; other schools may not allow it.
4
Note the analogy with garment hire
The commentary compares to hiring a garment without specifying the wearer. If the hirer then clothes a person, ignorance is removed and the rent is due.
Qiyas with a similar case of jahalah in manfa'ah.
5
Identify the difference with sale
In lease, rights like irrigation and path are automatically included as accessories because the contract is for use. In sale, these are not included unless mentioned because sale is for ownership of the corpus.
Thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in including ancillary rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة
ما يجوز من الإجارة: إن بين ما يزرع فيها أو قال على أن يزرع ما شاء، وللبناء والغرس فإن مضت المدة قلعهما وسلمها فارغة إلا أن يغرم المؤجر.
لأنها منفعة يقصد بالأراضي.
إن بين إلخ: لأن الأرض لما كانت تستأجر للزراعة وغيرها، وما يزرع فيها متفاوت، وبعضها يضر بالأرض، لزم يعين ما يزرع فيها كي لا تقع المنازعة، والمنازعة كما ترتفع بالتعيين كذلك ترتفع بتفويض الخيرة إليه، بأن قال: يزرع فيها ما شاء، وإن لم يعين ولم يفوض، فالإجارة فاسدة؛ للجهالة وتنقلب صحيحة بزرعها، ويجب المسمى لارتفاع الجهالة بعد الزرع كاستئجار ثوب لم يبين لابسه إذا ألبس شخصا، وإذا استأجر أحد أرضا فله الشرب والطريق؛ لأنها تنعقد للانتفاع، ولا انتفاع إلا بهما، فيدخلان تبعا، وليس هذا كالبيع، فإن المقصود منه ملك الرقبة، لا الانتفاع في الحال حتى جاز بيع الجحش والأرض السبخة دون إجارتهما، إلا أن يذكر الحقوق والمرافق، وإذا استأجر أرضا سنة على أن يزرع فيها ما شاء، فله أن يزرع فيها زرعين ربيعيا وخريفيا. (كذا في الفتح، البحر)
Book of Ijarah: What is permissible of leasing... If he specifies what is to be sown in it or says that he may sow what he wishes, and for building and planting, and if the term expires, he removes them and delivers it empty unless the lessor pays compensation... Because it is a utility intended with lands. 'If he specifies etc.': Because land is leased for cultivation and other things, and what is sown in it varies, and some harm the land, so it is necessary to specify what is sown in it to avoid dispute. And just as dispute is removed by specification, it is also removed by delegating choice to him, by saying: sow in it what you wish. And if he does not specify and does not delegate, the lease is void due to ignorance, and it becomes valid by sowing it, and the named rent is obligatory because ignorance is removed after sowing, like renting a garment without specifying its wearer when he clothes a person. And if one leases land, he has the right to irrigation and path because the contract is concluded for use, and there is no use except through them, so they are included as accessories. This is not like sale, for its purpose is ownership of the corpus, not current use, so it is permissible to sell a foal and swampy land but not to lease them, unless the rights and appurtenances are mentioned. And if one leases land for a year on condition that he sows what he wishes, he may sow two crops, spring and autumn. (Thus in al-Fath, al-Bahr)
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kitab al-Ijarah explaining the conditions for a valid lease of land for cultivation. The matn states that the lease is valid if the lessee specifies what crop will be sown, or if he delegates the choice to himself by saying 'I may sow what I wish.' The commentary explains the reasoning: because different crops have different effects on the soil, failing to specify leads to ignorance (jahalah) and potential dispute, rendering the contract void (fasid). However, if the lessee actually sows without specification, the contract becomes valid and the named rent is due, because the ignorance is removed by the act of sowing. Analogous to hiring a garment without specifying its wearer. The passage also notes that when leasing land, the lessee automatically gets rights to irrigation and a path, as these are necessary for use—unlike in sale where such rights are not included unless mentioned. Finally, if the lease is for a year with general permission, the lessee may plant two crops (spring and autumn), according to the relied-upon authorities. All examples and rulings are strictly from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijārah): A contract for transferring usufruct (manfa'ah) of a known asset for a known compensation.
جهالة (jahālah): Ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter, which renders a contract fasid.
مسمى (musammā): The named rent or compensation agreed upon in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the initial status of a land lease for cultivation when the crop is not specified and no delegation is given?
A) The lease is valid (sahih) from the start.
B) The lease is void (batil).
C) The lease is voidable (fasid) but becomes valid when the lessee sows.
D) The lease is suspended (mawquf) until the crop is specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a land lease without crop specification is initially fasid but becomes sahih upon sowing, with the named rent due.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
10 min
General Rule: Removal of Buildings and Trees at Lease End
General ruling on lease for building/planting and removal obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on removal of buildings and trees at lease end and explain the compensation alternative based on the source text.

Core Idea
Buildings and trees must be removed because they have no fixed end; but the lessor can pay compensation to avoid removal.

This lesson addresses the fundamental ruling for land leased for building or planting. At the end of the lease, the lessee generally must remove these improvements and return the land empty. However, Hanafi fiqh provides a balanced option: the lessor may pay compensation to keep them. We will explore the rationale, the exception for fruit-bearing plants, and the ruling for herbage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is removing buildings and trees at lease end. The ruling is wajib because they have no fixed end and leaving them harms the landowner.
Harm prevention is the underlying rationale.
2
Locate the main proof
The text states 'because they have no fixed end, so leaving them harms the landowner, so removal is obligatory.' This is a qiyas jali based on the principle of preventing harm.
No explicit Quran/hadith is cited; the reasoning is based on the nature of the contract.
3
Understand the compensation exception
The matn says 'Unless the lessor pays compensation.' If the lessor pays the value of the improvements as removed, the lessee is not obliged to remove. This is a mutual agreement that prevents waste.
Compensation is the value of the building/tree as if it were already removed (mumayyaz).
4
Condition for compensation
The land must decrease in value with removal. If it does not decrease, the lessor cannot compel the lessee to accept compensation; the lessee's consent is required.
This condition protects the lessee from being forced into an unfair transaction.
5
Apply to similar cases
Herbage (رطبة) that has no fixed end is treated like trees: removal is obligatory unless compensated. Fruit-bearing trees: if fruit is present at lease end, the tree remains with rent similar until harvest.
Crops with a known end (e.g., vegetables) are treated differently (covered in next lesson).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللبناء: عطف على للزارعة أي تصح إجارة الأرض للبناء والغرس. والغرس وهو بفتح الغين بمعنى المغروس وقد جاء فيه الكسر كذا في "المغرب". فإن مضت المدة: أي مدة العقد قلع المستأجر البناء والغرس، وسلم الأرض إلى المؤجر؛ لأنه لا نهاية لهما، ففي إبقائهما إضرار لصاحب الأرض، فوجب القلع إلا أن يكون في الغرس ثمرة، فتبقى بأجر المثل إلى حين الإدراك. (بحر وفتح) قلعهما إلخ : أي يجب على المستأجر القلع والتسليم، واستثنوا من هذه القاعدة أرض الوقف، فإنه ليس فيها على المستأجر القلع، وإن أبى الموقوف عليهم إلا القلع، قال في "الفتح": وفي "القنية": استأجر أرضا وقفا، وبنى فيها وغرس، ثم مضت مدة الإجارة، فللمستأجر أن يستبقيها بأجر المثل وإن لم يكن في ذلك ضرر، وإن أبى الموقوف عليهم إلا القلع، ليس لهم ذلك، لكنه مخالف لما في "الإسعاف" و"العمادية" من أنه إذا كان رفع البناء لا يضر بالوقف يرفعه الباني؛ لأنه ملكه ويجبر عليه، ولو بنى بإذن القيم كان له الرجوع بما أنفق على العمارة في غلة الوقف، وهي للوقف "حموي" عن "المقدسي"، وأقول ما ذكره في "الإسعاف" و"العمادية" يحمل على ما إذا كان إبقاء البناء يضر بالوقف، فلا يخالف حينئذ ما في "البحر" عن "القنية" "والخصاف"؛ لأنه مشروط بما إذا لم يكن في بقائه ضرر، انتهى ما في "الفتح".
إلا أن يغرم المؤجر يعني يجب على المستأجر القلع إلا إذا يغرم المؤجر قيمة الغرس أو البناء مقلوعا؛ لأنه يستحق القلع، فتقوم الأرض بدون البناء والغرس وتقوم وفيها بناء وغرس لصاحب الأرض أن يأمره بقلعه، فيضمن فضل ما بينهما، هذا إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع، وإن كانت لا تنقص وأراد أن يضمن له قيمته، ويكون له البناء، ليس له ذلك إلا أن يرضي صاحبه، قال في "البحر": وهذا الاستثناء المذكور في المتن راجع إلى لزوم القلع على المستأجر ، فأفاد أنه إذا رضي المؤجر بدفع القيمة لا يلزم المستأجر القلع، وهذا صحيح . مطلقا،

ما يجوز من الإجارة....

كالشجر والرطبة، والزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك، والدابة للركوب

سواء كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع أو لا، فلا حاجة إلى حمل كلام المصنف على ما إذا كانت تنقص بالقلع كما فعل الشارح تبعا لغيره لكن لا يتملكها المؤجر جبرا على المستأجر إلا إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع، وأما إذا كانت لا تنقص فلابد من رضاه البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٧] قيمته: أي قيمة كل واحد من الغرس والبناء. فيكون البناء إلخ : أي إذا رضي المؤجر بترك البناء أو الغرس لا يلزم المستأجر القلع وهذا الترك من المؤجر يكون عارية لأرضه إن كان بغير أجر، وإجارة إن كان بأجر، فقصره في "غاية البيان" على الأول مما لا ينبغي. [البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٧] والرطبة إلخ : [ المراد بها ما يبقى أصله وتقطف ثمره أو زهره] لأنه لما لم يكن لها نهاية معلومة، ففي إبقائها إضرار بصاحب الأرض، فيكون حكمه حكم الشجر ، قال في "الفتح": وإذا كان له نهاية معلومة كما في الفجل والجزر والباذنجان فينبغي أن يكون كالزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى نهاية كذا حرره المصنف في حواشي "الكنز" وقواه بما في معاملة "الخانية"، قلت: بقي لو له نهاية طويلة كالقصب، فيكون كالشجر، كما في "فتاوى ابن الحلبي". يترك إلخ: أي إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة قبل إدراكه يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك رعاية للجانبين؛ لأنه له نهاية معلومة بخلاف موت أحدهما قبل إدراكه فإنه يترك بالمسمى على حاله إلى الحصاد البحر الرائق: ٤٤٦/٧]
And for building: it is an adjunct to 'for cultivation', i.e., the lease of land for building and planting is valid. 'Planting' (ghars) is with a fatha on the ghayn meaning what is planted, and it is also pronounced with kasra as in al-Mughrib. 'If the term expires': i.e., the term of the contract, the lessee removes the building and planting and delivers the land to the lessor because they have no fixed end, and leaving them harms the landowner, so removal is obligatory unless there is fruit in the planting, in which case it remains with rent similar until harvest. (Bahr and Fath) 'Remove them etc.': i.e., it is obligatory on the lessee to remove and deliver, and they excepted from this rule waqf land, for the lessee is not obliged to remove, even if the beneficiaries refuse except removal. Al-Fath said: In al-Qunyah: one leased waqf land, built and planted, then the lease term expired; the lessee may keep them with rent similar even if there is no harm, and if the beneficiaries refuse except removal, they do not have that. But it contradicts what is in al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya that if removing the building does not harm the waqf, the builder removes it because it is his property and he is compelled, and if he built with permission of the custodian, he may recoup his expenses from the waqf's yield. I say: what is mentioned in al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya is carried on the case where keeping the building harms the waqf, so it does not contradict what is in al-Bahr from al-Qunyah and al-Khassaf because it is conditioned on there being no harm in its remaining. End from al-Fath. 'Unless the lessor pays compensation': i.e., removal is obligatory on the lessee unless the lessor pays the value of the planting or building as removed, because he deserves removal. Then the land is valued without the building and planting and with them; the landowner may order its removal, and then pays the difference. This is when the land decreases in value by removal; if it does not decrease and he wants to pay its value and have the building, he may not unless the lessee agrees. Al-Bahr said: This exception mentioned in the matn refers to the necessity of removal on the lessee, so it indicates that if the lessor agrees to pay the value, the lessee is not obliged to remove. This is correct absolutely. [Page 153] ... 'Its value as removed, and he owns it, or he agrees to leave it, so the building and tree are for this one and the land for that one' ... 'like trees and herbage, and crops are left with rent similar until harvest, and the animal for riding' ... 'whether the land decreases by removal or not, so there is no need to restrict the author's words to the case of decrease as the commentator did following others, but the lessor does not own them forcibly over the lessee unless the land decreases; if it does not decrease, his consent is required (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:445). 'Its value': i.e., the value of each of the planting and building. 'So the building etc.': i.e., if the lessor agrees to leave the building or planting, the lessee is not obliged to remove, and this leaving by the lessor is a loan for his land if without rent, and a lease if with rent; restricting it to the first in Ghayat al-Bayan is not appropriate. (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:445) 'And herbage etc.': what is intended is what retains its root and its fruit or flower is picked, because since it has no known end, leaving it harms the landowner, so its ruling is the same as trees. Al-Fath said: If it has a known end like radish, carrot, and eggplant, then it should be like crops left with rent similar until its end. The author discussed this in his glosses on al-Kanz and strengthened it with what is in Mu'amalat al-Khaniyyah. I say: if it has a long end like sugarcane, it is like trees, as in Fatawa Ibn al-Halabi. 'It is left etc.': i.e., if the lease term expires before it is ready, it is left with rent similar until it is ready, out of consideration for both sides, because it has a known end, unlike the death of one of them before readiness, where it is left with the named rent until harvest. (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:446)
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of land lease, now focusing on building and planting. The matn states that leasing land for building or planting trees is valid. At the end of the term, the lessee must remove these structures and return the land empty, unless the lessor chooses to pay compensation for their value as removed. The commentary explains that removal is required because buildings and trees have no fixed end, and leaving them would harm the landowner. An exception is made if the planting has fruit: it stays with rent until harvest. For waqf land, the lessee may keep the improvements with rent even if the beneficiaries object, provided no harm to the waqf—a ruling from al-Qunyah, with a different view from al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya that is reconciled by conditioning on harm. The compensation rule: if the land decreases in value by removal, the lessor can pay the difference to keep the improvements; if no decrease, the lessee's consent is needed. The passage also covers herbage (رطبة) which, like trees, has no fixed end; but crops with a fixed end (like vegetables) are left with rent until harvest. Sugarcane, having a long but finite end, is treated like trees. These details show the Hanafi approach of balancing the rights of lessor and lessee while preventing harm.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Removal of a structure or tree from the leased land at the end of the term.
رطبة (raṭbah): Herbage or plants that remain rooted and are repeatedly harvested (like perennial crops).
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Market rent or compensation for use, as opposed to the named rent in the original contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the lessor to compel the lessee to accept compensation for improvements?
A) The improvements must be fruit-bearing.
B) The land must decrease in value by removal.
C) The lessee must agree to any compensation.
D) The lease must be for an indefinite term.

Key Takeaway
Buildings and trees on leased land must be removed at lease end, but the lessor can avoid this by paying compensation if the land decreases in value with removal.

Special Cases: Waqf Land, Known-End Crops, and Sugarcane
Special rulings for waqf land and classification of crops by end duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings for waqf land, crops with fixed ends, and sugarcane to given scenarios, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on waqf.

Core Idea
Waqf land allows lessee to keep improvements with rent if no harm; crops with known ends stay with rent until harvest; sugarcane is treated like trees.

This lesson explores the exceptions and classifications within the lease rulings. Waqf land receives special treatment to protect the endowment's interest, leading to an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by conditioning on harm. Additionally, the passage distinguishes between plants with known finite ends (vegetables) and those with long or indefinite ends (sugarcane, herbage) to determine whether they remain with rent or must be removed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the waqf exception
For waqf land, the lessee may keep buildings and trees with rent similar (ujr al-mithl) even if the beneficiaries object, provided no harm to the waqf. This is from al-Qunyah.
A conflicting view from al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya says the lessee must remove if no harm; al-Bahr reconciles by conditioning on harm.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
There are two positions: (1) lessee may keep regardless if no harm (al-Qunyah), (2) lessee must remove if no harm (al-Is'af). Al-Bahr reconciles: the second applies only when keeping the building harms the waqf, so no real conflict.
The reconciliation ensures the lessee can keep improvements when it benefits the waqf.
3
Classify crops by end type
Crops with a known short end (radish, carrot, eggplant) are left with rent similar until harvest. They are like regular crops because their end is finite and within a short period.
This avoids harming the lessee's investment.
4
Classify sugarcane
Sugarcane, having a long but finite end, is treated like trees: removal is obligatory unless compensated. Its long duration makes it akin to a permanent improvement.
Fatawa Ibn al-Halabi supports this classification.
5
Summarize the classification rule
The key factor is the length of the end: short finite end → leave with rent similar; long or indefinite end → remove or compensate. This prevents both waste and harm.
The same principle applies to herbage (indefinite end) seen in Lesson 1.

Source Text

يكون قابلا للحصاد
سواء كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع أو لا، فلا حاجة إلى حمل كلام المصنف على ما إذا كانت تنقص بالقلع كما فعل الشارح تبعا لغيره لكن لا يتملكها المؤجر جبرا على المستأجر إلا إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع، وأما إذا كانت لا تنقص فلابد من رضاه البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٧] قيمته: أي قيمة كل واحد من الغرس والبناء. فيكون البناء إلخ : أي إذا رضي المؤجر بترك البناء أو الغرس لا يلزم المستأجر القلع وهذا الترك من المؤجر يكون عارية لأرضه إن كان بغير أجر، وإجارة إن كان بأجر، فقصره في "غاية البيان" على الأول مما لا ينبغي. [البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٧] والرطبة إلخ : [ المراد بها ما يبقى أصله وتقطف ثمره أو زهره] لأنه لما لم يكن لها نهاية معلومة، ففي إبقائها إضرار بصاحب الأرض، فيكون حكمه حكم الشجر ، قال في "الفتح": وإذا كان له نهاية معلومة كما في الفجل والجزر والباذنجان فينبغي أن يكون كالزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى نهاية كذا حرره المصنف في حواشي "الكنز" وقواه بما في معاملة "الخانية"، قلت: بقي لو له نهاية طويلة كالقصب، فيكون كالشجر، كما في "فتاوى ابن الحلبي". يترك إلخ: أي إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة قبل إدراكه يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك رعاية للجانبين؛ لأنه له نهاية معلومة بخلاف موت أحدهما قبل إدراكه فإنه يترك بالمسمى على حاله إلى الحصاد البحر الرائق: ٤٤٦/٧]
And for building: it is an adjunct to 'for cultivation', i.e., the lease of land for building and planting is valid. 'Planting' (ghars) is with a fatha on the ghayn meaning what is planted, and it is also pronounced with kasra as in al-Mughrib. 'If the term expires': i.e., the term of the contract, the lessee removes the building and planting and delivers the land to the lessor because they have no fixed end, and leaving them harms the landowner, so removal is obligatory unless there is fruit in the planting, in which case it remains with rent similar until harvest. (Bahr and Fath) 'Remove them etc.': i.e., it is obligatory on the lessee to remove and deliver, and they excepted from this rule waqf land, for the lessee is not obliged to remove, even if the beneficiaries refuse except removal. Al-Fath said: In al-Qunyah: one leased waqf land, built and planted, then the lease term expired; the lessee may keep them with rent similar even if there is no harm, and if the beneficiaries refuse except removal, they do not have that. But it contradicts what is in al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya that if removing the building does not harm the waqf, the builder removes it because it is his property and he is compelled, and if he built with permission of the custodian, he may recoup his expenses from the waqf's yield. I say: what is mentioned in al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya is carried on the case where keeping the building harms the waqf, so it does not contradict what is in al-Bahr from al-Qunyah and al-Khassaf because it is conditioned on there being no harm in its remaining. End from al-Fath. 'Unless the lessor pays compensation': i.e., removal is obligatory on the lessee unless the lessor pays the value of the planting or building as removed, because he deserves removal. Then the land is valued without the building and planting and with them; the landowner may order its removal, and then pays the difference. This is when the land decreases in value by removal; if it does not decrease and he wants to pay its value and have the building, he may not unless the lessee agrees. Al-Bahr said: This exception mentioned in the matn refers to the necessity of removal on the lessee, so it indicates that if the lessor agrees to pay the value, the lessee is not obliged to remove. This is correct absolutely. [Page 153] ... 'Its value as removed, and he owns it, or he agrees to leave it, so the building and tree are for this one and the land for that one' ... 'like trees and herbage, and crops are left with rent similar until harvest, and the animal for riding' ... 'whether the land decreases by removal or not, so there is no need to restrict the author's words to the case of decrease as the commentator did following others, but the lessor does not own them forcibly over the lessee unless the land decreases; if it does not decrease, his consent is required (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:445). 'Its value': i.e., the value of each of the planting and building. 'So the building etc.': i.e., if the lessor agrees to leave the building or planting, the lessee is not obliged to remove, and this leaving by the lessor is a loan for his land if without rent, and a lease if with rent; restricting it to the first in Ghayat al-Bayan is not appropriate. (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:445) 'And herbage etc.': what is intended is what retains its root and its fruit or flower is picked, because since it has no known end, leaving it harms the landowner, so its ruling is the same as trees. Al-Fath said: If it has a known end like radish, carrot, and eggplant, then it should be like crops left with rent similar until its end. The author discussed this in his glosses on al-Kanz and strengthened it with what is in Mu'amalat al-Khaniyyah. I say: if it has a long end like sugarcane, it is like trees, as in Fatawa Ibn al-Halabi. 'It is left etc.': i.e., if the lease term expires before it is ready, it is left with rent similar until it is ready, out of consideration for both sides, because it has a known end, unlike the death of one of them before readiness, where it is left with the named rent until harvest. (al-Bahr al-Raiq 7:446)
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of land lease, now focusing on building and planting. The matn states that leasing land for building or planting trees is valid. At the end of the term, the lessee must remove these structures and return the land empty, unless the lessor chooses to pay compensation for their value as removed. The commentary explains that removal is required because buildings and trees have no fixed end, and leaving them would harm the landowner. An exception is made if the planting has fruit: it stays with rent until harvest. For waqf land, the lessee may keep the improvements with rent even if the beneficiaries object, provided no harm to the waqf—a ruling from al-Qunyah, with a different view from al-Is'af and al-Imadiyya that is reconciled by conditioning on harm. The compensation rule: if the land decreases in value by removal, the lessor can pay the difference to keep the improvements; if no decrease, the lessee's consent is needed. The passage also covers herbage (رطبة) which, like trees, has no fixed end; but crops with a fixed end (like vegetables) are left with rent until harvest. Sugarcane, having a long but finite end, is treated like trees. These details show the Hanafi approach of balancing the rights of lessor and lessee while preventing harm.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Removal of a structure or tree from the leased land at the end of the term.
رطبة (raṭbah): Herbage or plants that remain rooted and are repeatedly harvested (like perennial crops).
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Market rent or compensation for use, as opposed to the named rent in the original contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is sugarcane treated at the end of a lease?
A) It is left with rent similar until harvest like vegetables.
B) It must be removed unless the lessor compensates, like trees.
C) It may remain without any rent.
D) It must be removed immediately regardless of compensation.

Key Takeaway
For waqf land, improvements stay with rent if no harm; for crops, the classification depends on whether the end is known and short or long (like sugarcane).

Leasing Animals for Riding and Carrying: Validity and Customary Permissions
States validity of leasing animals for transport and related disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid and void leases of animals and garments, and apply the Hanafi view on combining riding and carrying based on custom.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) can override strict contractual terms in Hanafi ijara, allowing the lessee to ride an animal leased for carrying without extra liability.

In Hanafi law, a lease contract is valid only if the intended benefit is known and customary. This passage explores when leasing an animal for transport is valid, and delves into an intra-madhhab dispute: may a lessee ride the animal while carrying goods? The preferred view relies on custom (urf) and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for valid ijara
The lessor must intend a utility that is customary and known. Leasing an animal just to show off is void because the benefit is not intended from the object.
This condition applies to all leases of objects.
2
Apply the default rule on permitted use
If a lessee leases for carrying, he may also ride, because 'riding is called carrying'. But if leased for riding, he cannot load goods; if he does, no rent is due for the extra use.
Riding is considered a form of carrying oneself.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Bakr al-Razi says that if the lessee both carries and rides the animal at the same time, he cannot combine them; if the animal perishes, he is liable. Abu al-Layth says he can combine due to custom, and is not liable.
This dispute is about simultaneous use, not sequential.
4
See how custom (urf) and istihsan resolve the dispute
Custom makes the combined use implicitly permitted (dalalah). Istihsan overrides the strict qiyas that would hold the lessee liable. This is the mufta bihi view.
Hanafi usul accepts urf as a source when no explicit text contradicts.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern analogy
Leasing a car for transporting goods: does the lessee have the right to also drive it for personal travel? The same principles apply—custom determines the permitted use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدابة: عطف على الدور أي صح إجارة الدابة للركوب إلخ : تصح إجارة الدابة للركوب والحمل وإجارة الثوب للبس؛ لأن المنفعة الحاصلة منهما مقصودة معهودة معلومة فيصح عقد الإجارة، وإنما قيد إجارة الدابة بالركوب والحمل، وكذا الثوب باللبس؛ لما مر من أن الإجارة تنعقد على منفعة مقصودة من العين، فعلى هذا لو استأجر دابة لا للركوب والحمل، بل ليجنبها أو ليربطها على باب داره ليرى الناس أن له فرسا، أو استأجر ثوبا لا للبس، بل ليزين بيته به أو حانوته، فالإجارة فاسدة.
وكلما
ووجهه أن هذه المنفعة ليست بمقصودة من العين ولو استأجر الدابة للحمل فله الركوب، وإن استأجرها للركوب لا يحمل عليها، وإن حمل فلا أجر عليه؛ لأن الركوب يسمى حملا لا عكسه. قال في "البحر" [٤٤٧/٧]: فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على أن من استأجرها للحمل له أن يركبها، لكنهم اختلفوا في ما إذا استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها حنطة من موضع إلى منزله يوما إلى الليل وكان يحمل الحنطة إلى منزله، كان يركبها هل له رجع ذلك أم لا ؟ وقال أبو بكر الرازي: ليس له الجمع بينهما، فإذا عطبت الدابة في هذه الصورة ضمنها، وقال الفقيه أبو الليث: له ذلك، ولا يضمنه في الاستحسان؛ لأن العادة جرت فيما بين الناس بذلك، فصار مأذونا فيه دلالة، وإن لم يأذن بالإفصاح. (بحر وفتح مع زيادة وتوضيح من
المحشي)
And the animal: it is an adjunct to 'houses', i.e., the lease of an animal for riding etc. is valid. The lease of an animal for riding and carrying and the lease of a garment for wearing are valid, because the utility obtained from them is intended, customary, and known, so the lease contract is valid. The lease of an animal is restricted to riding and carrying, and similarly the garment to wearing, because, as has passed, the lease is concluded for a utility intended from the object. Thus, if one leases an animal not for riding or carrying but to lead it or tie it at his door so people see he has a horse, or leases a garment not for wearing but to decorate his house or shop, the lease is void. And the reason is that this utility is not intended from the object. If one leases an animal for carrying, he may ride it; if he leases it for riding, he may not carry on it, and if he carries, there is no rent for that, because riding is called carrying but not the reverse. Al-Bahr said: The upshot is that they agree that one who leases for carrying may ride it, but they differ if one leases an animal to carry wheat from a place to his home from morning to night, and he carries the wheat to his home and also rides it—does he have that right or not? Abu Bakr al-Razi said: he may not combine both; if the animal perishes in this case, he is liable. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: he may do so and is not liable in istihsan, because custom among people permits it, so it is implicitly permitted even if not explicitly stated. (Bahr and Fath with addition and clarification from the marginalia)
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of leasing animals for riding and carrying. The matn establishes that leasing an animal for transportation is valid because the utility is well-known and intended. If the lease is for a non-utility purpose (e.g., just to show off), the contract is void. The text then addresses a key detail: if one leases an animal for carrying goods, he may also ride it, because riding is a form of carrying oneself. But if he leases it for riding, he may not load goods on it; if he does, he pays no additional rent. The commentary clarifies a dispute among Hanafi scholars: Abu Bakr al-Razi holds that if the lessee both carries goods and rides simultaneously, he cannot combine both, and if the animal perishes, he is liable. However, al-Faqih Abu al-Layth says he may combine due to custom ('urf), and is not liable in istihsan. The latter is the preferred view, as custom effectively grants implied permission. This illustrates the Hanafi use of custom (urf) and istihsan in contract law.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Preferring a ruling that deviates from qiyas due to custom, necessity, or equity.
دلالة (dalālah): Implicit indication or permission derived from custom, as opposed to explicit wording.
عرف (urf): Custom or common practice that can affect legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person leases a donkey to carry his goods, can he also ride the donkey according to the preferred Hanafi view?
A) No, he can only carry goods.
B) Yes, and if the donkey perishes, he is not liable.
C) Yes, but if it perishes, he must compensate.
D) He cannot ride unless explicitly permitted in the contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijara, custom (urf) can create implied permissions—such as the lessee's right to ride an animal leased for carriage—and istihsan overrides strict qiyas in favor of common practice.

Validity of Hiring Animals for Riding and Garments for Wearing
States validity and invalid cases for ijarah of animals and garments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid ijarah regarding the nature of the benefit and apply it to distinguish valid from invalid hirings.

Core Idea
Ijarah is valid only for the primary intended benefit of an item, not for accessory or show purposes.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a key principle in ijarah: the hired item must be used for its customary, intended purpose. The author explains that hiring an animal for riding or carrying, and a garment for wearing, are valid because these benefits are well-known and purposeful. Conversely, using these items for decoration or display makes the contract invalid. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that ijarah of an animal for riding/carrying and of a garment for wearing is valid (sahih). Identify the act as 'ijarah' and the ruling as 'valid'.
2
Locate the condition for validity
The validity depends on the benefit being 'intended' (maqsudah), 'customary' (ma'hudah), and 'known' (ma'lumah). Highlight these adjectives.
3
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The author uses 'because' (li-anna) to give the reason: the benefit obtained from these uses is intended. Ijarah is only concluded on a benefit that is the primary purpose of the asset.
This is a talil argument — the ruling follows from the nature of the contract.
4
Apply to invalid cases
If the animal is hired to be kept aside or tied for show, or the garment to decorate a house, the ijarah becomes invalid (fasid). The benefit is not intended from the property.
The principle applies uniformly: any non-primary use invalidates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدابة للركوب عطف على الدور أي صح إجارة الدابة للركوب والحمل وإجارة الثوب للبس؛ لأن المنفعة الحاصلة منهما مقصودة معهودة معلومة فيصح عقد الإجارة، وإنما قيد إجارة الدابة بالركوب والحمل، وكذا الثوب باللبس؛ لما مر من أن الإجارة تنعقد على منفعة مقصودة من العين، فعلى هذا لو استأجر دابة لا للركوب والحمل، بل ليجنبها أو ليربطها على باب داره ليرى الناس أن له فرسا، أو استأجر ثوبا لا للبس، بل ليزين بيته به أو حانوته، فالإجارة فاسدة. وكلما ووجهه أن هذه المنفعة ليست بمقصودة من العين
And the animal for riding is a conjunction to 'houses' meaning: the ijarah of an animal for riding and carrying is valid, and the ijarah of a garment for wearing is valid; because the benefit obtained from them is intended, customary, and known, so the ijarah contract is valid. He only restricted the ijarah of an animal to riding and carrying, and likewise the garment to wearing, because it has passed that ijarah is concluded on an intended benefit from the property. So on this basis, if someone hires an animal not for riding and carrying, but to keep it aside or tie it at the door of his house so that people see he has a horse, or hires a garment not for wearing, but to decorate his house or shop with it, then the ijarah is invalid. And everything... and its reason is that this benefit is not intended from the property.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the validity of hiring animals and garments. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq declares that hiring an animal for riding or carrying and hiring a garment for wearing are valid because the benefit (utility) is recognized, customary, and known. The commentary explains that the validity hinges on the benefit being 'intended from the property' (مقصودة من العين). Thus, if someone hires an animal merely to tie it at his door for show, or hires a garment to decorate his house, the contract is invalid (fasid) because the benefit is not a primary purpose of such items. The passage also alludes to a principle that ijarah is only on intended benefits, not on accessory uses.

Key Terms
منفعة مقصودة (manfa'ah maqsudah): A benefit that is the primary and intended use of the item
منفعة معهودة معلومة (manfa'ah ma'hudah ma'lumah): A benefit that is customary and known (specific in nature and amount)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is hiring a garment to decorate a house invalid (fasid)?
A) Because the garment is not suitable for decoration.
B) Because the benefit is not the intended use of the garment.
C) Because the contract is not in writing.
D) Because the rental period is unspecified.

Key Takeaway
Ijarah is valid only when the benefit is the primary, intended purpose of the hired item; otherwise the contract is fasid.

Combining Riding and Carrying in Animal Hire
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining riding and carrying in animal hire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on whether a hirer may ride while carrying loads and apply the relied-upon position based on custom (urf) and istihsan.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) can imply permission in contracts, overriding strict terms when it is universal practice.

Hire contracts (ijara) specify a particular benefit—either riding or carrying. But what happens when a hirer does both? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi on whether combining is allowed. The debate hinges on the role of custom as an implied condition. You will learn how the Hanafi school uses dalalah (implicit indication) and istihsan to accommodate customary practice.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Read the opening sentence: if hired for carrying, the hirer may ride; if hired for riding, he may not carry. Understand the reasoning: riding is a type of carrying, not vice versa.
This rule is agreed upon by all.
2
Identify the contested scenario
Focus on the scenario where a person hires an animal to carry wheat for a full day and rides it while carrying. This is where the ikhtilaf occurs.
The dispute is about combining both uses simultaneously.
3
Present Abu Bakr al-Razi's position
Abu Bakr al-Razi holds that combining is not permitted; the contract is for carrying only. If the animal perishes, the hirer is liable (daman) because he is a transgressor.
He follows strict adherence to contractual terms.
4
Present Abu al-Layth's position
Abu al-Layth holds that combining is permitted by istihsan, based on custom (urf). People commonly ride while carrying, so the contract implicitly allows it (dalalah). No liability if the animal perishes.
Custom overrides strict analogy.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu al-Layth's view, as cited in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Fath al-Qadir. Custom is widespread and gives implicit permission.
This illustrates the Hanafi preference for istihsan over qiyas jali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلما ووجهه أن هذه المنفعة ليست بمقصودة من العين ولو استأجر الدابة للحمل فله الركوب، وإن استأجرها للركوب لا يحمل عليها، وإن حمل فلا أجر عليه؛ لأن الركوب يسمى حملا لا عكسه. قال في "البحر" [٤٤٧/٧]: فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على أن من استأجرها للحمل له أن يركبها، لكنهم اختلفوا في ما إذا استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها حنطة من موضع إلى منزله يوما إلى الليل وكان يحمل الحنطة إلى منزله، كان يركبها هل له رجع ذلك أم لا ؟ وقال أبو بكر الرازي: ليس له الجمع بينهما، فإذا عطبت الدابة في هذه الصورة ضمنها، وقال الفقيه أبو الليث: له ذلك، ولا يضمنه في الاستحسان؛ لأن العادة جرت فيما بين الناس بذلك، فصار مأذونا فيه دلالة، وإن لم يأذن بالإفصاح. (بحر وفتح مع زيادة وتوضيح من المحشي)
And everything... and its reason is that this benefit is not intended from the property. And if someone hires an animal for carrying, he may ride it; if he hires it for riding, he may not carry on it, and if he carries, there is no rent for him because riding is called carrying, not the opposite. He said in al-Bahr [7/447]: The conclusion is that they agreed that whoever hires an animal for carrying may ride it, but they differed regarding if someone hires an animal to carry wheat from a place to his home for a day until night, and he was carrying the wheat to his home, and he rode it, does he have the right to combine or not? Abu Bakr al-Razi said: He does not have the right to combine both, and if the animal perishes in this case, he is liable. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: He has that right, and he is not liable by istihsan, because custom among people is such, so it becomes implicitly permitted, even if not explicitly authorized. (Bahr and Fath with addition and clarification from the annotator)
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage contains an intra-Hanafi dispute. It first states a general rule: if hired for carrying, the hirer may also ride, but if hired for riding, he may not carry. The reason is that riding is a type of carrying, but not vice versa. The main ikhtilaf concerns a specific scenario: a person hires an animal to carry wheat to his home for a full day, and he simultaneously rides the animal while carrying the wheat. Abu Bakr al-Razi holds that this combination is not permitted; if the animal perishes, the hirer is liable (daman). Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, relying on istihsan (juristic preference), holds that the hirer may combine because custom allows riding while carrying, effectively giving implicit permission. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu al-Layth's view due to custom.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalalah): Implicit indication; permission inferred from custom
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding strict analogy due to custom or necessity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a person hires an animal to carry wheat and rides it as well?
A) It is not permitted, and he is liable if the animal perishes.
B) It is permitted, and he is not liable because custom allows it.
C) It is permitted only if the owner explicitly allows riding.
D) It is not permitted, but he is not liable if the animal perishes.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custom (urf) can give implicit permission in hire contracts, overriding strict terms when the practice is widespread, and this is upheld by istihsan.

Ruling on Structures and Plants on Leased Land after Lease Expiry
Explains options for lessor and lessee after lease expiry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the options for lessor and lessee regarding buildings and plants left on leased land after the lease expires, and distinguish between plants with a known end (like crops) and those without (like trees).

Core Idea
Lessor can only force sale of structures if removal damages land; otherwise consent required.

When a lease ends, what happens to buildings and plants the lessee has placed on the land? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that balances the rights of both parties. This lesson unpacks the key distinctions: whether removal damages the land, and whether the plants have a definite harvest season. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly advising on lease-end disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its range of rulings
The act is the lessee leaving buildings or plants on the land after the lease ends. The ruling is not a single hukm but a set of options depending on circumstances.
The passage outlines two main scenarios: lessor agrees to leave them, or lessor forces sale.
2
Isolate the condition for forced sale
The lessor can force the lessee to sell the structures/plants at their removal value only if removing them would damage the land. If no damage, the lessor must obtain the lessee's consent.
This is the key principle from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
3
Classify plants by end-point
Plants with no known end (like ratbah grass that regrows) are treated like trees. Plants with a known end (crops like radishes, carrots) are left with ujr al-mithl until harvest. Long-end plants like sugarcane are also treated like trees.
Qiyas (analogy) is used to extend the ruling of trees to similar plants.
4
Apply the exception for death of a party
If the lease ends because of death of one party before the crop is ripe, the crop is left with the original named rent until harvest, not ujr al-mithl.
This is due to the contract's continuity after death in some cases.
5
Understand the voluntary leave option
If the lessor agrees to let the structures remain, it is either a loan (without rent) or a new lease (with rent). The lessee is not compelled to remove.
This is from the commentary clarifying the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة ما يجوز من الإجارة.... قيمته مقلوعا، ويتملكه، أو يرضى بتركه فيكون البناء والشجر لهذا، والأرض لهذا حال كونه مقلوعا المؤجر البناء والغرس أي المستأجر كالشجر والرطبة، والزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك. سواء كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع أو لا، فلا حاجة إلى حمل كلام المصنف على ما إذا كانت تنقص بالقلع كما فعل الشارح تبعا لغيره لكن لا يتملكها المؤجر جبرا على المستأجر إلا إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع، وأما إذا كانت لا تنقص فلابد من رضاه البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٧] قيمته: أي قيمة كل واحد من الغرس والبناء. فيكون البناء إلخ : أي إذا رضي المؤجر بترك البناء أو الغرس لا يلزم المستأجر القلع وهذا الترك من المؤجر يكون عارية لأرضه إن كان بغير أجر، وإجارة إن كان بأجر، فقصره في "غاية البيان" على الأول مما لا ينبغي. [البحر الرائق: ٤٤٥/٧] والرطبة إلخ : [ المراد بها ما يبقى أصله وتقطف ثمره أو زهره] لأنه لما لم يكن لها نهاية معلومة، ففي إبقائها إضرار بصاحب الأرض، فيكون حكمه حكم الشجر ، قال في "الفتح": وإذا كان له نهاية معلومة كما في الفجل والجزر والباذنجان فينبغي أن يكون كالزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى نهاية كذا حرره المصنف في حواشي "الكنز" وقواه بما في معاملة "الخانية"، قلت: بقي لو له نهاية طويلة كالقصب، فيكون كالشجر، كما في "فتاوى ابن الحلبي". يترك إلخ: أي إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة قبل إدراكه يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك رعاية للجانبين؛ لأنه له نهاية معلومة بخلاف موت أحدهما قبل إدراكه فإنه يترك بالمسمى على حاله إلى الحصاد البحر الرائق: ٤٤٦/٧]
Book of Ijarah. What is permissible in ijarah... Its value as removed, and he owns it, or he agrees to leave it, so the building and trees are for this [the lessor] and the land is for this [the lessor] while it is removed. The lessor [owns] the building and planting, i.e., the lessee [owns them]? like trees and moist plants, and the crop is left with ujr al-mithl until it ripens. Whether the land decreases by removal or not, there is no need to carry the author's statement only on the case where it decreases by removal as the commentator did following others, but the lessor does not own it forcibly against the lessee unless the land decreases by removal. As for when it does not decrease, his consent is necessary. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/445. Its value: i.e., the value of each one of the planting and building. 'So the building etc.': i.e., if the lessor agrees to leave the building or planting, the lessee is not compelled to remove, and this leaving by the lessor is a loan for his land if without rent, and ijarah if with rent. Restricting it to the first in Ghayat al-Bayan is not appropriate. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/445] 'And the moist plants etc.': [What is meant is what remains with its root and its fruit or flower is plucked] because it has no known end, so leaving it harms the landowner, so its ruling is like trees. He said in al-Fath: If it has a known end like radish, carrot, and eggplant, it should be like crop left with ujr al-mithl until its end. The author verified this in his glosses on al-Kanz and supported it with what is in Mu'amalat al-Khaniyah. I say: What remains if it has a long end like sugarcane, it is like trees, as in Fatawa Ibn al-Halabi. 'Leaves etc.': i.e., if the ijarah period ends before it is ripe, it is left with ujr al-mithl until it ripens, out of consideration for both sides; because it has a known end, unlike the death of one of them before it ripens, then it is left with the named rent as it is until harvest. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 7/446]
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about what happens to structures and plants left on leased land after the lease expires. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that the lessee may either remove them (and the lessor pays their value in that state) and then owns them, or the lessor agrees to leave them in place. In the latter case, the buildings and trees belong to the lessor while the land remains his. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the lessor cannot force the lessee to sell the buildings; he can only take them at value if the land would be damaged by removal. If removal does not harm the land, the lessor needs the lessee's consent to keep them. The commentator also explains that plants like grass (ratbah) that regrow after cutting are treated like trees because they have no definite end. However, crops with a known season (like radishes, carrots, eggplant) are left with a fair rent until harvest. Sugarcane, having a long but definite end, is treated like trees. The passage also distinguishes between leaving plants after lease expiry versus after death of a party: if one dies before harvest, the crop is left with the named rent, not ujr al-mithl, due to the contract's continuation.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): The fair market rental rate for similar property
القلع (al-qal'): Removal of structures or plants from the land
الرطبة (al-ratbah): Moist plants that regrow after cutting

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes plants that are left with ujr al-mithl from those treated like trees?
A) Whether the plant is annual or perennial
B) Whether the plant has a known end or not
C) The size of the plant
D) The plant's market value

Key Takeaway
The lessor can only compel sale of buildings/plants if removal damages the land; otherwise, the lessee's consent is required, and plants are classified by whether they have a known harvest end.

Hiring a Riding Animal or Garment: The Role of Specifying the User
Ruling on hiring with or without specifying user

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hiring an animal or garment with and without specifying the user, and apply the rule to determine liability when the hirer violates a restriction.

Core Idea
In Hanafi ijarah, the user must be specified or left free; violation makes the hirer liable if the item varies by user.

The validity of an ijarah contract often depends on clarity of the benefit. For items like riding animals and garments, the identity of the user can significantly affect the benefit. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq distinguishes between unrestricted hire (itlaq), which is valid and gives the hirer freedom to choose the user, and restricted hire, where the hirer must adhere to the specified user. Violation of a restriction renders the hirer a transgressor, imposing liability for any damage and forfeiting the right to rent. The passage also explores an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding tents, demonstrating how the principle of variation by user is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage to find the two fundamental cases: unrestricted hire (mutlaq) and restricted hire (muqayyad). In unrestricted hire, the lessor allows the hirer to choose any user; in restricted hire, a specific person is named.
2
Understand the principle of variation
The passage states that animals and garments 'differ by the user' (yakhtalifu bi al-musta'mil). This means the benefit varies with the person riding or wearing. Therefore, specification is required to avoid uncertainty (jahalah).
If the item does not vary by user (e.g., a house), the ruling may differ.
3
Apply the consequence of violation
If the hirer violates a valid restriction (e.g., allows a different person to ride), he becomes a transgressor. If the item perishes, he must pay compensation (dhaman), and no rent is due because liability and rent cannot combine. If the item survives, still no rent is owed.
This is derived from the statement: 'wa la ajra alayhi idha atibat li'adam ijtimai al-daman wa al-ajr'.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on tents
The passage extends the ruling to a tent (fustat). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if the hirer violates a restriction by giving the tent to another who erects and destroys it, the hirer is liable because the benefit varies (erection, location, pegging). Muhammad disagrees, comparing it to a house where the benefit does not vary. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
This dispute illustrates how the principle is applied to new cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجوز من الإجارة.... والثوب للبس، فإن أطلق أركب وألبس من شاء، وإن قيد براكب أو لابس، فخالف ضمن. ومثله ما يختلف بالمستعمل، وما لا يختلف به بطل. والحمل: بشرط أن يبين من يركبها أو ما يحمل عليها، وكذا الثوب يشترط فيه البيان. ويكفي في استيجار الثوب للبس التمكن منه وإن لم يلبس، كالسكنى تجب الأجرة بنفس القبض وإن لم يسكن. بخلاف ما إذا استأجر الدابة ليركبها حيث لا يكفي التمكن حتى لو أمسكها في منزله في المصر لا يجب الأجر، ويضمن لو هلكت. فإن أطلق أي بأن قال: على أن يركب أو يلبس من شاء، وهو المراد بالإطلاق، لا أن يستأجر الدابة للركوب ويطلقه إطلاقا فإنه لا يجوز بل تفسد الإجارة، ووجه عدم الجواز الجهالة، فلو أركبها أو ركب بنفسه في صورة الجهالة، وجب عليه المسمى استحسانا، وفي القياس عليه أجر المثل. وجه الاستحسان: أن المفسد هو الجهالة، وقد زال فيزول الفساد، لأنا نجعل التعيين في الانتهاء كالتعيين في الابتداء، ولا ضمان بالهلاك لأنه غير متعد. والحاصل أن الدابة والثوب يختلفان باختلاف المستعمل، فلا يجوز الإجارة إلا بالتعيين أو بأن يشترط يفعل ما شاء. وإذا صحت الإجارة عند التعميم تعين أول راكب أو لابس. براكب بأن قال على أن يركبه فلان، فخالف ضمن: لأن التقييد مفيد لتفاوت الناس في الركوب واللبس، فإذا خالف صار متعديا فيضمن ولا أجر عليه إذا عطبت لعدم اجتماع الضمان والأجر، وكذا لا أجر عليه إن سلمت. واستفيد من كلام المصنف أنه إذا قيد ليس له الإجارة والإعارة كما أنه إذا أعم له ذلك، وليس له الإيداع في الأول ولو لضرورة دون الثاني. ومثله الحكم في كل ما يختلف باختلاف المستعمل في كونه يضمن إذا عطبت المخالفة، والتقييد كالفسطاط إذا استأجره أحد وقيده المؤجر فدفعه إلى غيره إجارة أو إعارة مع نصبه وسكن فيه حتى تلف ضمن عند أبي يوسف وكذا عند أبي حنيفة لأن الناس يتفاوتون في نصبه واختيار مكانه وضرب أوتاده، وعند محمد لا يضمن لأنه كالسكنى عنده فصار كالدار.
What is permissible from hiring... and the garment for wearing. If he left it unrestricted, he may ride and clothe whomever he wishes. And if he restricted it to a rider or a wearer, then he violated, he is liable. And similar is what differs by the user, and what does not differ by it becomes void. Carrying is conditional that he specify who rides it or what is carried on it; likewise the garment requires specification. It suffices in hiring a garment for wearing the ability to use it even if not worn, like a dwelling: the rent is due by mere possession even if not inhabited. Contrary to the case of hiring an animal for riding where ability is not sufficient, so if he keeps it in his house in the city, the rent is not due, and he is liable if it perishes. As for 'if left unrestricted': meaning he said that he may ride or clothe whomever he wishes—this is the intended meaning of unrestricted; not that he hires the animal for riding and leaves it completely unrestricted, for that is not permissible, rather the contract becomes void. The reason for impermissibility is uncertainty (jahalah). If he then rode it or rode himself in the case of uncertainty, the named wage is due by istihsan, whereas by qiyas the comparable wage is due. The basis of istihsan: the defect is uncertainty, and it has gone, so the defect goes; we make designation at the end like designation at the beginning. There is no liability for destruction because he is not transgressing (ghayr muta'addin). The conclusion: the animal and garment differ by the user, so hiring is not permissible except with designation or by stipulating that he may do as he wishes. When the contract is valid under generalization, the first rider or wearer becomes designated. 'By a rider': meaning he said that so-and-so will ride it; then he violated, he is liable. Because the restriction is beneficial due to the variation of people in riding and wearing; when he violated, he becomes a transgressor and is liable, and no rent is due if it perishes due to the non-combination of liability and rent. Similarly, no rent is due if it remains safe. It is understood from the author's words that when he restricts, he may not sub-hire nor lend, just as when he makes general he may do so. He may not deposit in the first case even if necessity, unlike the second. The same ruling applies to everything that differs by the user: he is liable if destruction occurs by violation. An example of restriction is the tent: if one hires it and the lessor restricts, then he hands it to another as hire or loan, and he erects it and lives in it until it is destroyed—he is liable according to Abu Yusuf and also according to Abu Hanifa, because people differ in its erection, choice of location, and striking of pegs. According to Muhammad, he is not liable, because it is like a dwelling according to him, so it becomes like a house.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with the permissibility of hiring an animal for riding or a garment for wearing. The core ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) unrestricted hire (itlaq), where the lessor says 'ride or wear whomever you wish', which is valid and gives the hirer freedom to choose the user; (2) restricted hire, where the lessor specifically names a particular rider or wearer. If the hirer violates that restriction, he becomes a transgressor and is liable (dhaman) if the item perishes, and no rent is due. The author explains that this distinction is based on the variation of benefit by the user: different people affect the animal or garment differently. Similarly, for loading, the type of load must be specified. The passage also addresses a subsidiary issue: the hirer's ability to sub-hire or lend. When unrestricted, the hirer may sub-hire or lend; when restricted, he may not even deposit (wadi'ah) with another. The commentary then discusses the example of a tent (fustat), noting an intra-Hanafi disagreement: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if the lessor restricts the tent to a specific person, and the hirer gives it to another who erects and destroys it, the hirer is liable because the benefit varies with the user. Muhammad argues no liability, analogizing it to a house where the benefit does not vary by user. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract of hire for a specific benefit in exchange for known compensation.
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Uncertainty in the contract that leads to invalidity if it affects the benefit.
الضمان (al-dhaman): Liability for compensation when property is destroyed due to transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man hires a garment for wearing without specifying a wearer (mutlaq), and then gives it to someone else to wear and it is destroyed, is the hirer liable?
A) Yes, because he violated the contract.
B) No, because the hire was unrestricted and he was free to let anyone wear it.
C) Yes, because he did not wear it himself.
D) No, because the wearer is liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah for items varying by user, the user must be specified or the contract must allow any user; violation of restriction incurs liability and forfeits rent.

Specifying Type and Quantity in Hire of Animals
Ruling on load specification and extra rider liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on permitted load variations in animal hire and determine liability when an extra rider causes the animal's death.

Core Idea
Loading a different but equally heavy item is excused by istihsan if not harmful; taking an extra rider incurs half liability if the animal can carry two.

When you hire an animal to carry a specific load, you may carry the same or lighter weight, but not a more harmful item. What if you carry a different material of the same weight? The Hanafi school uses istihsan to excuse you—unless the load is actually more harmful. Then, the rule of 'irdaf (extra rider) carefully distinguishes liability based on the animal's capacity and the rider's control. Let's walk through these principles from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline rule
The hirer is entitled to a quantified benefit. Loading the exact specified load (e.g., one kor of wheat) or a lighter load of the same type is permissible.
This follows the general principle of mu'tamad: you cannot exceed the contracted benefit.
2
Analyze the qiyas vs istihsan exception
By qiyas, loading a different kind (e.g., iron instead of cotton) would incur liability even if weight matches. But istihsan overrides: since the restriction on type is not beneficial in this context (same weight, no harm), there is no liability.
The key is whether the change is harmful (adar). If harmful, liability stands.
3
Examine the irdaf scenario and liability tiers
Hirer takes an extra rider. If the animal can carry two and the extra rider holds himself, hirer pays half the animal's value. If cannot carry two, full value. If extra rider is a child who cannot hold himself, liability proportional to his weight.
The half liability is specific to irdaf; carrying on shoulder incurs full liability.
4
Determine the owner's choice and recourse
Owner can choose to hold the hirer or the extra rider liable. If hirer pays, he cannot recover from extra rider. If extra rider pays, he can recover from hirer only if he was a hirer himself (not a borrower).
Recourse depends on the legal relationship between hirer and extra rider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سمى نوعا وقدرا ككر بر له حمل مثله وأخف لا أضر كالملح، وإن عطبت بالإرداف ضمن النصف، أي نصف قيمة الدابة. وفي المسألة: أن من استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها، وسمى حملا معينا، فله أن يحمل عليها ما هو مثل كر الحنطة أو أخف منه لا أضر. والأصل: أن من استحق منفعة مقدرة بالعقد فاستوفى تلك المنفعة أو مثلها أو أقل منها جاز، وإن استوفى أكثر منها لم يجز. ومن الأضر ما لو استأجرها لحمل قطن معلوم فحمل مثل وزنه حديدا. والقياس أن يضمن إذا حمل خلاف الجنس كيف ما كان، إلا أنه في الاستحسان لا يضمن لأن التقييد إنما يعتبر إذا كان مفيدا ولا فائدة هنا. وإن عطبت: صورة المسألة أن رجلا استأجر دابة ليركبها فأردف معه رجلا فعطبت الدابة، فإن كانت الدابة تطيق حمل اثنين وكان الرديف مستمسكا بنفسه ضمن المستأجر نصف قيمة الدابة ولا اعتبار للثقل. وإن كانت لا تطيق ضمن جميع القيمة. وإن كان الرديف صغيرا لا يستمسك بنفسه ضمن بقدر ثقله. وإنما قيد ضمان نصف القيمة بالإرداف لأنه لو حمله على عاتقه وعطبت ضمن جميع القيمة وإن كانت تطيق. وإطلاق المصنف الإرداف يشمل ما إذا أردف ولد الناقة المستأجرة الذي ولدته بعد الإجارة وإن كان ملك صاحبها. ضمن النصف أي ضمن المستأجر نصف قيمة الدابة، ثم المالك بالخيار إن شاء ضمن المستأجر وإن شاء ضمن ذلك الرجل، فإن ضمن المستأجر لا يرجع هو على ذلك الرجل مستأجرا كان أو مستعيرا، وإن ضمن ذلك الرجل رجع هو على المستأجر إن كان ذلك الرجل مستأجرا، وإن كان مستعيرا لا يرجع. وينقل أنه يجب جميع الأجر إذا هلك بعد بلوغ المقصد مع تضمين نصف القيمة.
And if he specified type and quantity, like one kor of wheat, he may load its like or lighter, not more harmful like salt. And if it perishes due to taking an extra rider (irdaf), he is liable for half, meaning half the value of the animal. In this issue: one who hires an animal to carry something and specifies a particular load, he may carry what is like that kor of wheat or lighter, not more harmful. The principle: one who is entitled to a quantified benefit by contract, if he takes that benefit or its like or less, it is permissible; if he takes more, it is not. Among the more harmful is if he hires for a known weight of cotton and loads iron of the same weight. By qiyas he would be liable if he loads a different kind regardless, but by istihsan he is not liable because the restriction is only considered if beneficial, and there is no benefit here. And if it perishes: the scenario: a man hires an animal to ride, then he takes another behind him (irdaf) and the animal perishes. If the animal can carry two and the extra rider holds himself, the hirer is liable for half the value, and weight is not considered. If it cannot carry two, he is liable for the full value. If the extra rider is a child who cannot hold himself, he is liable according to his weight. The limitation of half liability to 'irdaf' is because if he carries him on his shoulders and it perishes, he is liable for the full value even if it could carry both. The author's general wording of 'irdaf' includes when he takes the young of the hired she-camel that was born after the hire, even if it belongs to the owner. 'Liable for half' means the hirer is liable for half the value of the animal; then the owner has a choice: if he wishes, he may hold the hirer liable, or he may hold that man liable. If he holds the hirer liable, the hirer does not have recourse against that man, whether that man was a hirer or a borrower. If he holds that man liable, that man has recourse against the hirer if that man was a hirer; if he was a borrower, he does not have recourse. It is reported that the full rent is due if the animal perishes after reaching the destination, along with liability for half the value.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq covers two related rules in hiring animals. First, when the hirer specifies the type and quantity of load (e.g., one kor of wheat), he may load the same or lighter, but not more harmful (like loading iron instead of cotton). The principle is that he is entitled to a quantified benefit; taking more is not allowed. By qiyas, loading a different kind would incur liability, but by istihsan, if the load is the same weight or less and not more harmful, there is no liability because the restriction is not beneficial. Second, the rule of 'irdaf (taking an extra rider). If a hirer of a riding animal takes another behind him, and the animal perishes, the hirer's liability depends on the animal's capacity and the extra rider's ability to hold himself. If the animal can carry two and the extra rider holds himself, the hirer pays half the animal's value; if not, full value. The owner has the choice to hold the hirer or the extra rider liable. The hirer cannot recover from the extra rider if he paid, but the extra rider can recover from the hirer if the extra rider was also a hirer (not a borrower). Additionally, if the animal perishes after reaching the destination, the full rent is due along with the half liability. The passage also notes that if the extra rider is the young of the hired animal born after the contract, it is considered an unauthorized additional load.

Key Terms
الإرداف (al-irdaf): Taking an extra rider behind the main rider on the same animal.
الضمان (al-dhaman): Liability for compensation when the animal is destroyed due to unauthorized use.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases to achieve a more equitable outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man hires a horse to carry 100 kg of rice. He loads 100 kg of sand (same weight, not more harmful). The horse dies. According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling?
A) He is liable because sand is a different type.
B) He is not liable because the load is not more harmful.
C) He is liable for half the value.
D) He is liable for the full value.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi animal hire, loading a different but equally heavy non-harmful item is excused by istihsan, while taking an extra rider (irdaf) incurs half liability if the animal can carry two and the rider holds himself.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
8 min
Carrying Specified Load: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Ruling on carrying same or lighter load in hired animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to determine liability when a hired animal carries a load of a different genus but equivalent weight.

Core Idea
Harmless deviations from contract terms are excused by istihsan when the specification has no benefit.

In Hanafi fiqh, hiring an animal for a specific load involves balancing contractual rights with practical flexibility. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq demonstrates that while the default analogy (qiyas) would impose liability for any deviation, juristic preference (istihsan) permits carrying a different genus of the same weight because the weight—not the genus—is the material term when no harm occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the contract
The act is hiring a beast to carry a specified load (e.g., one kurr of wheat). Note that the contract specifies both genus and quantity.
The contract gives the hirer a measured benefit (manfa'ah muqaddarah).
2
State the general principle
The commentary states: if a person is entitled to a measured benefit, utilizing that benefit, its like, or less is permissible; utilizing more is not.
This principle applies to both similar and different genera.
3
Apply to a straightforward case
If the specified load is wheat, the hirer may carry wheat for another (like) or a lighter grain (less). No liability.
Similarly for land cultivation (e.g., planting a similar crop).
4
Examine the problematic case: different genus, same weight
E.g., hired for cotton but carries iron of same weight. Qiyas would impose liability because the genus is different, violating the contract. However, Hanafi istihsan exempts this.
Why? The specification of genus is only considered if it is beneficial (mufid). Here, weight is the functional criterion, and the animal is not harmed.
5
Recognize the role of istihsan
Istihsan overrides the strict qiyas due to lack of benefit in the genus restriction. This is a distinctive Hanafi method.
Contrast with other schools that may hold the hirer liable for any deviation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سمى نوعا وقدرا ككر بر له حمل مثله وأخف لا أضر كالملح. وإن سمي إلخ: هذه مسألة أخرى بيانها: أن من استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها، وسمى حملا معينا بأن قال: أحمل عليها كر حنطة، فله أن يحمل عليها ما هو مثل كر الحنطة، أو أخف منه لا آخر كالملح وغيره، والأصل فيها: أن من استحق منفعة مقدرة بالعقد، فاستوفى تلك المنفعة أو مثلها أو أقل منها جاز، وإن استوفى أكثر منها لم يجز، فله أن يحمل كر حنطة لغيره لو استأجرها لحمل كر حنطة؛ لأنه مثله، وعلى هذا زراعة الأراضي لو عين نوعا للزراعة، له أن يزرع مثله وأخف منه، لا أضر، ومن الأضر ما لو استأجرها لحمل قطن معلوم، فحمل مثل وزنه حديدا أو مثل وزن الحنطة قطنا أو تبنا أو حطبا، وكان القياس في هذه المسألة أن يضمن إذا حمل إليها خلاف الجنس كيف ما كان مثله أو أخف أو أضر؛ لأنه خالف العقد إلا أنه في الاستحسان لا يضمن؛ لأن التقييد إنما يعتبر إذا كان مفيدا ولا فائدة هنا. ملخص ما في البحر والعيني مع (توضيح)
And if he specifies a type and quantity, like a kurr of wheat, he may carry its like and lighter, not harmful like salt. And [the commentary says] 'And if he specifies etc.': This is another issue. Its explanation: that if a person hires a beast to carry on it, and specifies a particular load such that he says 'I will carry on it a kurr of wheat', then he may carry on it what is like the kurr of wheat, or lighter than it, not another like salt and others. And the principle in it is: that whoever is entitled to a measured benefit by contract, and he utilizes that benefit or its like or less, it is permissible; and if he utilizes more than it, it is not permissible. Thus he may carry a kurr of wheat for another if he hired it for carrying a kurr of wheat, because it is its like. And similarly for land cultivation: if he specifies a type for cultivation, he may plant its like and lighter, not harmful. And among harmful is if he hired it for carrying a known quantity of cotton, then he carried iron of its weight, or cotton of the weight of wheat, or straw, or firewood. And the analogy in this issue is that he would be liable if he carried a different genus, whether like, lighter, or harmful, because he violated the contract. However, by istihsan he is not liable because the restriction is only considered if it is beneficial, and there is no benefit here. Summarized from al-Bahr and al-Ayni with (Tawdih).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq along with its commentary. The ruling: if a person hires an animal to carry a specified load (e.g., one kurr of wheat), he may carry the same load or a lighter one, but not a harmful load like salt (which damages the animal). The commentary explains the principle (asl): the contract entitles to a measured benefit; utilizing the same, its like, or less is permissible; more is not. Applying this, if he hires for wheat, he may carry wheat for another, or a similar grain. The commentary then discusses a problematic case: if he hires for a specific weight of cotton but carries iron of the same weight, the default analogy (qiyas) would require liability because he violated the contract (different genus). However, Hanafi istihsan rules no liability because the specification of genus in such a case is not beneficial—the weight is what matters. Thus the condition is disregarded. This exemplifies the Hanafi preference for substantive benefit over literal terms.

Key Terms
كر (kurr): A unit of weight, approximately 720 kg (varies by region). Used as an example of a specified load.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence or consideration of public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person hires a donkey to carry one kurr of wheat, and instead carries one kurr of barley (lighter but different genus), is he liable if the donkey is harmed?
A) Yes, because he violated the contract specification of genus.
B) Yes, because barley is lighter and thus different weight.
C) No, because the weight is the same and the specification is not beneficial.
D) No, because barley is lighter and thus permissible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, harmless deviations from a specified load in a hire contract are permitted by istihsan, overriding the strict qiyas, when the contractual term has no tangible benefit.
